class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Nuclear fusion</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Fusion_(disambiguation)" title="Fusion (disambiguation)">Fusion (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the science behind nuclear fusion. For its use in producing energy, see <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion power</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3860" data-file-width="4044" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/220px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/330px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg/440px-The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Sun_by_the_Atmospheric_Imaging_Assembly_of_NASA%27s_Solar_Dynamics_Observatory_-_20100819.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-sequence_star" title="Main-sequence star">main-sequence star</a>, and thus generates its <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> by nuclear fusion of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> nuclei into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. In its core, the Sun fuses 620 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> of hydrogen each second.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/400px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/600px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/800px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a> curve. The formation of nuclei with masses up to <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">Iron-56</a> releases energy, while forming those elements that are heavier requires energy input. This is because the nuclei below Iron-56 have high binding energies, while the heavier ones have lower binding energies, as illustrated above.</div></div></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox Collider">RHIC</a> &#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">LHC</a></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Category:Nuclear_physicists" title="Category:Nuclear physicists">Scientists</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Alvarez</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Becquerel</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Bethe</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Aage_Bohr" title="Aage Bohr">A.Bohr</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">N.Bohr</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">Chadwick</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/John_Cockcroft" title="John Cockcroft">Cockcroft</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Ir&#232;ne Joliot-Curie">Ir.Curie</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Fr&#233;d&#233;ric Joliot-Curie">Fr.Curie</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pi.Curie</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Sk&#322;odowska-Curie</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Davisson" title="Clinton Davisson">Davisson</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Fermi</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Hahn</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/J._Hans_D._Jensen" title="J. Hans D. Jensen">Jensen</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Lawrence" title="Ernest Lawrence">Lawrence</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Goeppert-Mayer" title="Maria Goeppert-Mayer">Mayer</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Meitner</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oliphant" title="Mark Oliphant">Oliphant</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">Oppenheimer</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Alexandru_Proca" title="Alexandru Proca">Proca</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Mills_Purcell" title="Edward Mills Purcell">Purcell</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Isidor_Isaac_Rabi" title="Isidor Isaac Rabi">Rabi</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Rutherford</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Soddy</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Strassmann</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Le&#243; Szil&#225;rd">Szil&#225;rd</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Teller" title="Edward Teller">Teller</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">Thomson</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Walton" title="Ernest Walton">Walton</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Wigner" title="Eugene Wigner">Wigner</a></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Nuclear_physics" title="Template:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nuclear_physics" title="Template talk:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_physics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chemistry" title="Nuclear chemistry">nuclear chemistry</a>, <b>nuclear fusion</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">reaction</a> in which two or more <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> are combined to form one or more different atomic nuclei and subatomic particles (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protons" title="Protons">protons</a>). The difference in mass between the reactants and products is manifested as either the release or absorption of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. This difference in mass arises due to the difference in atomic "<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">binding energy</a>" between the atomic nuclei before and after the reaction. Fusion is the process that powers active or "<a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>" <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">high magnitude</a> stars.\n</p><p>A fusion process that produces a nucleus lighter than <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">iron-56</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62">nickel-62</a> will generally yield a net energy release. These elements have the smallest mass per nucleon and the largest <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> per <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a>, respectively. Fusion of light elements toward these releases energy (an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> process), while a fusion producing nuclei heavier than these elements will result in energy retained by the resulting nucleons, and the resulting reaction is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a>. The opposite is true for the reverse process, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>. This means that the lighter elements, such as hydrogen and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helium_fusion" title="Helium fusion">helium</a>, are in general more fusible; while the heavier elements, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_235" title="Uranium 235">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium_239" title="Plutonium 239">plutonium</a>, are more fissionable. The extreme <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysical</a> event of a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> can produce enough energy to fuse nuclei into elements heavier than iron.\n</p><p>In 1920, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> suggested hydrogen-helium fusion could be the primary source of stellar energy. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" title="Quantum tunneling">Quantum tunneling</a> was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hund" title="Friedrich Hund">Friedrich Hund</a> in 1929, and shortly afterwards <a href="/wiki/Robert_d%27Escourt_Atkinson" title="Robert d\'Escourt Atkinson">Robert Atkinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Houtermans" title="Fritz Houtermans">Fritz Houtermans</a> used the measured masses of light elements to show that large amounts of energy could be released by fusing small nuclei. Building on the early experiments in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutation</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>, laboratory fusion of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">hydrogen isotopes</a> was accomplished by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oliphant" title="Mark Oliphant">Mark Oliphant</a> in 1932. In the remainder of that decade, the theory of the main cycle of nuclear fusion in stars were worked out by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a>.  Research into fusion for military purposes began in the early 1940s as part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>.  Fusion was accomplished in 1951 with the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Item" title="Greenhouse Item">Greenhouse Item</a> nuclear test.  Nuclear fusion on a large scale in an explosion was first carried out on November 1, 1952, in the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a> test.\n</p><p>Research into developing controlled <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a> for civil purposes began in earnest in the 1940s, and it continues to this day.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" <span class="toctext">Criteria and candidates for terrestrial reactions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Neutronicity,_confinement_requirement,_and_power_density"><span plasmas</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Mathematical_description_of_cross_section"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Mathematical description of cross section</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Fusion_under_classical_physics"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Fusion under classical physics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Parameterization_of_cross_section"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Parameterization of cross section</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Formulas_of_fusion_cross_sections"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Formulas of fusion cross sections</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Process">Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/220px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/330px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg/440px-Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium-tritium_fusion.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fusion of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> with <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> creating <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a>, freeing a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>, and releasing 17.59 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">MeV</a> as kinetic energy of the products while a corresponding amount of mass disappears, in agreement with <i>kinetic E</i> = &#916;<i>mc</i><sup>2</sup>, where <i>&#916;</i>m is the decrease in the total rest mass of particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shultis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shultis-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The release of energy with the fusion of light elements is due to the interplay of two opposing forces: the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>, which combines together protons and neutrons, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulomb_force" title="Coulomb force">Coulomb force</a>, which causes protons to repel each other. Protons are positively charged and repel each other by the Coulomb force, but they can nonetheless stick together, demonstrating the existence of another, short-range, force referred to as nuclear attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Light nuclei (or nuclei smaller than iron and nickel) are sufficiently small and proton-poor allowing the nuclear force to overcome repulsion. This is because the nucleus is sufficiently small that all nucleons feel the short-range attractive force at least as strongly as they feel the infinite-range Coulomb repulsion. Building up nuclei from lighter nuclei by fusion releases the extra energy from the net attraction of particles. <a href="/wiki/Iron_peak" title="Iron peak">For larger nuclei</a>, however, no energy is released, since the nuclear force is short-range and cannot continue to act across  longer atomic length scales. Thus, energy is not released with the fusion of such nuclei; instead, energy is required as input for such processes.\n</p><p>Fusion powers <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and produces virtually all elements in a process called <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a>. The Sun is a main-sequence star, and, as such, generates its energy by nuclear fusion of hydrogen nuclei into helium. In its core, the Sun fuses 620 million metric tons of hydrogen and makes 606 million metric tons of helium each second. The fusion of lighter elements in stars releases energy and the mass that always accompanies it. For example, in the fusion of two hydrogen nuclei to form helium, 0.7% of the mass is carried away in the form of kinetic energy of an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> or other forms of energy, such as electromagnetic radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulletin1950_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-bulletin1950-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It takes considerable energy to force nuclei to fuse, even those of the lightest element, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. When accelerated to high enough speeds, nuclei can overcome this electrostatic repulsion and brought close enough such that the attractive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> is greater than the repulsive Coulomb force. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_force" title="Strong force">strong force</a> grows rapidly once the nuclei are close enough, and the fusing nucleons can essentially "fall" into each other and the result is fusion and net energy produced. The fusion of lighter nuclei, which creates a heavier nucleus and often a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_neutron" title="Free neutron">free neutron</a> or proton, generally releases more energy than it takes to force the nuclei together; this is an <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic process</a> that can produce self-sustaining reactions.\n</p><p>Energy<span id="energy"></span> released in most <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> is much larger than in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a>, because the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> that holds a nucleus together is greater than the energy that holds <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> to a nucleus. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a> gained by adding an electron to a hydrogen nucleus is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6982217896002232000&#9824;</span>13.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a></span>&#8212;less than one-millionth of the <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6988281983061712000&#9824;</span>17.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">MeV</a></span> released in the <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> (D&#8211;T) reaction shown in the adjacent diagram. The complete conversion of one <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> of matter would release 9&#215;10<sup>13</sup> joules of energy. Fusion reactions have an <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> many times greater than <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>; the reactions produce far greater energy per unit of mass even though <i>individual</i> fission reactions are generally much more energetic than <i>individual</i> fusion ones, which are themselves millions of times more energetic than chemical reactions. Only <a href="/wiki/Direct_energy_conversion" title="Direct energy conversion">direct conversion</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass into energy</a>, such as that caused by the <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilatory</a> collision of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, is more energetic per unit of mass than nuclear fusion.\n</p><p>Research into using <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">fusion</a> for the production of electricity has been pursued for over 60 years. Successful accomplishment of controlled fusion has been stymied by scientific and technological difficulties; nonetheless, important progress has been made. At present, controlled fusion reactions have been unable to produce break-even (self-sustaining) controlled fusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The two most advanced approaches for it are magnetic confinement (toroid designs) and inertial confinement (laser designs).\n</p><p>Workable designs for a toroidal reactor that theoretically will deliver ten times more fusion energy than the amount needed to heat plasma to the required temperatures are in development (see <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a>). The ITER facility is expected to finish its construction phase in 2019. It will start commissioning the reactor that same year and initiate plasma experiments in 2020, but is not expected to begin full deuterium-tritium fusion until 2027.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The US <a href="/wiki/National_Ignition_Facility" title="National Ignition Facility">National Ignition Facility</a>, which uses laser-driven <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement fusion</a>, was designed with a goal of <a href="/wiki/Break-even" title="Break-even">break-even</a> fusion; the first large-scale laser target experiments were performed in June 2009 and ignition experiments began in early 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-programsNIF_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-programsNIF-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion_in_stars">Nuclear fusion in stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nuclear fusion in stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FusionintheSun.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="678" decoding="async" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/250px-FusionintheSun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/375px-FusionintheSun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/FusionintheSun.svg/500px-FusionintheSun.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FusionintheSun.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton-proton chain reaction">proton-proton chain reaction</a>, branch&#160;I, dominates in stars the size of the Sun or smaller.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNO_Cycle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/250px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/375px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/CNO_Cycle.svg/500px-CNO_Cycle.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNO_Cycle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> dominates in stars heavier than the Sun.</div></div></div>\n<p>An important fusion process is the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> that powers <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and the Sun. In the 20th century, it was recognized that the energy released from nuclear fusion reactions accounted for the longevity of stellar heat and light. The fusion of nuclei in a star, starting from its initial hydrogen and helium abundance, provides that energy and synthesizes new nuclei as a byproduct of the fusion process.  Different reaction chains are involved, depending on the mass of the star (and therefore the pressure and temperature in its core).\n</p><p>Around 1920, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> anticipated the discovery and mechanism of nuclear fusion processes in <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, in his paper <i>The Internal Constitution of the Stars</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eddington_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-eddington-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eddington2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-eddington2-9">[9]</a></sup> At that time, the source of stellar energy was a complete mystery; Eddington correctly speculated that the source was fusion of hydrogen into helium, liberating enormous energy according to <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">Einstein\'s equation</a> <i>E = mc<sup>2</sup></i>. This was a particularly remarkable development since at that time fusion and thermonuclear energy, and even that stars are largely composed of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>), had not yet been discovered. Eddington\'s paper, based on knowledge at the time, reasoned that:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><ol><li>The leading theory of stellar energy, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Contraction_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Contraction hypothesis (page does not exist)">contraction hypothesis</a>, should cause stars\' rotation to visibly speed up due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a>. But observations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cepheid" title="Cepheid">Cepheid</a> variable stars showed this was not happening.</li>\n<li>The only other known plausible source of energy was conversion of matter to energy; Einstein had shown some years earlier that a small amount of matter was equivalent to a large amount of energy.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Francis_William_Aston" title="Francis William Aston">Francis Aston</a> had also recently shown that the mass of a <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> atom was about 0.8% less than the mass of the four hydrogen atoms which would, combined, form a helium atom, suggesting that if such a combination could happen, it would release considerable energy as a byproduct.</li>\n<li>If a star contained just 5% of fusible hydrogen, it would suffice to explain how stars got their energy. (We now know that most \'ordinary\' stars contain far more than 5% hydrogen)</li>\n<li>Further elements might also be fused, and other scientists had speculated that stars were the "crucible" in which light elements combined to create heavy elements, but without more accurate measurements of their <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic masses</a> nothing more could be said at the time.</li></ol></dd></dl>\n<p>All of these speculations were proven correct in the following decades.\n</p><p>The primary source of solar energy, and similar size stars, is the fusion of hydrogen to form helium (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain" title="Proton-proton chain">proton-proton chain</a> reaction), which occurs at a solar-core temperature of 14 million kelvin. The net result is the fusion of four <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> into one <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>, with the release of two <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> (which changes two of the protons into neutrons), and energy. In heavier stars, the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> and other processes are more important. As a star uses up a substantial fraction of its hydrogen, it begins to synthesize heavier elements. The heaviest elements are synthesized by fusion that occurs as a more massive star undergoes a violent <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> at the end of its life, a process known as <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Requirements">Requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Requirements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A substantial energy barrier of electrostatic forces must be overcome before fusion can occur. At large distances, two naked nuclei repel one another because of the repulsive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a> between their <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">positively charged</a> protons. If two nuclei can be brought close enough together, however, the electrostatic repulsion can be overcome by the quantum effect in which nuclei can <a href="/wiki/Quantum_tunnelling#Nuclear_fusion_in_stars" title="Quantum tunnelling">tunnel</a> through coulomb forces.\n</p><p>When a <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> is added to a nucleus, the nuclear force attracts it to all the other nucleons of the nucleus (if the atom is small enough), but primarily to its immediate neighbours due to the short range of the force. The nucleons in the interior of a nucleus have more neighboring nucleons than those on the surface. Since smaller nuclei have a larger surface area-to-volume ratio, the binding energy per nucleon due to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> generally increases with the size of the nucleus but approaches a limiting value corresponding to that of a nucleus with a diameter of about four nucleons. It is important to keep in mind that nucleons are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" title="Quantum physics">quantum objects</a>. So, for example, since two neutrons in a nucleus are identical to each other, the goal of distinguishing one from the other, such as which one is in the interior and which is on the surface, is in fact meaningless, and the inclusion of quantum mechanics is therefore necessary for proper calculations.\n</p><p>The electrostatic force, on the other hand, is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">inverse-square force</a>, so a proton added to a nucleus will feel an electrostatic repulsion from <i>all</i> the other protons in the nucleus. The electrostatic energy per nucleon due to the electrostatic force thus increases without limit as nuclei atomic number grows.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="1186" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/350px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/525px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg/700px-Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fusion_forces_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a> between the positively charged nuclei is repulsive, but when the separation is small enough, the quantum effect will tunnel through the wall.  Therefore, the prerequisite for fusion is that the two nuclei be brought close enough together for a long enough time for quantum tunnelling to act.</div></div></div>\n<p>The net result of the opposing electrostatic and strong nuclear forces is that the binding energy per nucleon generally increases with increasing size, up to the elements <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, and then decreases for heavier nuclei. Eventually, the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> becomes negative and very heavy nuclei (all with more than 208 nucleons, corresponding to a diameter of about 6 nucleons) are not stable. The four most tightly bound nuclei, in decreasing order of <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> per nucleon, are <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">62</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron-58" title="Iron-58"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">58</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">56</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-60" title="Nickel-60"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Even though the <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nickel" title="Isotopes of nickel">nickel isotope</a>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">62</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, is more stable, the <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iron" title="Isotopes of iron">iron isotope</a> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">56</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is an <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">order of magnitude</a> more common. This is due to the fact that there is no easy way for stars to create <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">62</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> through the alpha process.\n</p><p>An exception to this general trend is the <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a> nucleus, whose binding energy is higher than that of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, the next heaviest element. This is because protons and neutrons are <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>, which according to the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> cannot exist in the same nucleus in exactly the same state. Each proton or neutron\'s energy state in a nucleus can accommodate both a spin up particle and a spin down particle. Helium-4 has an anomalously large binding energy because its nucleus consists of two protons and two neutrons, so all four of its nucleons can be in the ground state. Any additional nucleons would have to go into higher energy states.  Indeed, the helium-4 nucleus is so tightly bound that it is commonly treated as a single quantum mechanical particle in nuclear physics, namely, the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>.\n</p><p>The situation is similar if two nuclei are brought together. As they approach each other, all the protons in one nucleus repel all the protons in the other. Not until the two nuclei actually come close enough for long enough so the strong <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> can take over (by way of tunneling) is the repulsive electrostatic force overcome. Consequently, even when the final energy state is lower, there is a large energy barrier that must first be overcome. It is called the <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">Coulomb barrier</a>.\n</p><p>The Coulomb barrier is smallest for isotopes of hydrogen, as their nuclei contain only a single positive charge. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diproton" title="Diproton">diproton</a> is not stable, so neutrons must also be involved, ideally in such a way that a helium nucleus, with its extremely tight binding, is one of the products.\n</p><p>Using <a href="/wiki/Tritium#Deuterium" title="Tritium">deuterium-tritium</a> fuel, the resulting energy barrier is about 0.1&#160;MeV. In comparison, the energy needed to remove an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> is 13.6&#160;eV, about 7500 times less energy. The (intermediate) result of the fusion is an unstable <sup>5</sup>He nucleus, which immediately ejects a neutron with 14.1&#160;MeV. The recoil energy of the remaining <sup>4</sup>He nucleus is 3.5&#160;MeV, so the total energy liberated is 17.6&#160;MeV. This is many times more than what was needed to overcome the energy barrier.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/300px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/450px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Fusion_rxnrate.svg/600px-Fusion_rxnrate.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fusion_rxnrate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fusion reaction rate increases rapidly with temperature until it maximizes and then gradually drops off. The DT rate peaks at a lower temperature (about 70&#160;keV, or 800 million kelvin) and at a higher value than other reactions commonly considered for fusion energy.</div></div></div>\n<p>The reaction <b><a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a></b> &#963; is a measure of the probability of a fusion reaction as a function of the relative velocity of the two reactant nuclei. If the reactants have a distribution of velocities, e.g. a thermal distribution, then it is useful to perform an average over the distributions of the product of cross section and velocity. This average is called the \'reactivity\', denoted &lt;&#963;v&gt;. The reaction rate (fusions per volume per time) is &lt;&#963;v&gt; times the product of the reactant number densities:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle f=n_{1}n_{2}\\langle \\sigma v\\rangle .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle f=n_{1}n_{2}\\langle \\sigma v\\rangle .}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="f=n_{1}n_{2}\\langle \\sigma v\\rangle ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0b9a8d14268990bd84ad6e3d04a8fd2f2f044370" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.188ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>If a species of nuclei is reacting with a nucleus like itself, such as the DD reaction, then the product <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{1}n_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{1}n_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_{1}n_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a70ec72e416c0e2120ab2480be2bf67cc40817bd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.898ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> must be replaced by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle (1/2)n^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle (1/2)n^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="(1/2)n^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85e75cfa5d70ab77768fedfb8c7957cc6fd3808" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.746ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>.\n</p><p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\langle \\sigma v\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\langle \\sigma v\\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\langle \\sigma v\\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c32624c6fcf974ef4aa9bfc7a9e691757fbd523f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.267ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> increases from virtually zero at room temperatures up to meaningful magnitudes at temperatures of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_E-15_J" title="1 E-15 J">10</a>&#8211;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_E-14_J" title="1 E-14 J">100</a>&#160;keV. At these temperatures, well above typical <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionization</a> energies (13.6&#160;eV in the hydrogen case), the fusion reactants exist in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">plasma</a> state.\n</p><p>The significance of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\langle \\sigma v\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>&#963;<!-- &#963; --></mi>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\langle \\sigma v\\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\langle \\sigma v\\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c32624c6fcf974ef4aa9bfc7a9e691757fbd523f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.267ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> as a function of temperature in a device with a particular energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confinement_time" title="Confinement time">confinement time</a> is found by considering the <a href="/wiki/Lawson_criterion" title="Lawson criterion">Lawson criterion</a>.  This is an extremely challenging barrier to overcome on Earth, which explains why fusion research has taken many years to reach the current advanced technical state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawson_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-lawson-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_for_achieving_fusion">Methods for achieving fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Methods for achieving fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion power</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermonuclear_fusion">Thermonuclear fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Thermonuclear fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">Thermonuclear fusion</a></div>\n<p>If matter is sufficiently heated (hence being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">plasma</a>), fusion reactions may occur due to collisions with extreme thermal kinetic energies of the particles. Thermonuclear weapons produce what amounts to an uncontrolled release of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_energy" title="Fusion energy">fusion energy</a>. Controlled thermonuclear fusion energy has yet to be achieved.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_confinement_fusion">Inertial confinement fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Inertial confinement fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Inertial confinement fusion</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">Inertial confinement fusion</a> (<b>ICF</b>)  is a method aimed at releasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_energy" title="Fusion energy">fusion energy</a> by heating and compressing a fuel target, typically a pellet containing <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_electrostatic_confinement">Inertial electrostatic confinement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Inertial electrostatic confinement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">Inertial electrostatic confinement</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Inertial_electrostatic_confinement" title="Inertial electrostatic confinement">Inertial electrostatic confinement</a> is a set of devices that use an electric field to heat ions to fusion conditions.  The most well known is the <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a>.  Starting in 1999, a number of amateurs have been able to do amateur fusion using these homemade devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>  Other IEC devices include: the <a href="/wiki/Polywell" title="Polywell">Polywell</a>, MIX POPS<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and Marble concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beam-beam_or_beam-target_fusion">Beam-beam or beam-target fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Beam-beam or beam-target fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If the energy to initiate the reaction comes from <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">accelerating</a> one of the nuclei, the process is called <i>beam-target</i> fusion; if both nuclei are accelerated, it is <i>beam-beam</i> fusion.\n</p><p>Accelerator-based light-ion fusion is a technique using particle accelerators to achieve particle kinetic energies sufficient to induce light-ion fusion reactions. Accelerating light ions is relatively easy, and can be done in an efficient manner&#8212;requiring only a vacuum tube, a pair of electrodes, and a high-voltage transformer; fusion can be observed with as little as 10 kV between the electrodes. The key problem with accelerator-based fusion (and with cold targets in general) is that fusion cross sections are many orders of magnitude lower than Coulomb interaction cross sections. Therefore, the vast majority of ions expend their energy emitting <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a> radiation and the ionization of atoms of the target. Devices referred to as sealed-tube <a href="/wiki/Neutron_generator" title="Neutron generator">neutron generators</a> are particularly relevant to this discussion. These small devices are miniature particle accelerators filled with deuterium and tritium gas in an arrangement that allows ions of those nuclei to be accelerated against hydride targets, also containing deuterium and tritium, where fusion takes place, releasing a flux of neutrons. Hundreds of neutron generators are produced annually for use in the petroleum industry where they are used in measurement equipment for locating and mapping oil reserves.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muon-catalyzed_fusion">Muon-catalyzed fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Muon-catalyzed fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Muon-catalyzed_fusion" title="Muon-catalyzed fusion">Muon-catalyzed fusion</a> is a fusion process that occurs at ordinary temperatures. It was studied in detail by <a href="/wiki/Steven_E._Jones" title="Steven E. Jones">Steven Jones</a> in the early 1980s. Net energy production from this reaction has been unsuccessful because of the high energy required to create <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muons</a>, their short 2.2&#160;&#181;s <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a>, and the high chance that a muon will bind to the new <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> and thus stop catalyzing fusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_principles">Other principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TCV_vue_gen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/220px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/330px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TCV_vue_gen.jpg/440px-TCV_vue_gen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TCV_vue_gen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Tokamak_%C3%A0_configuration_variable" title="Tokamak &#224; configuration variable">Tokamak &#224; configuration variable</a></i>, research fusion reactor, at the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique_F%C3%A9d%C3%A9rale_de_Lausanne" title="&#201;cole Polytechnique F&#233;d&#233;rale de Lausanne">&#201;cole Polytechnique F&#233;d&#233;rale de Lausanne</a> (Switzerland).</div></div></div>\n<p>Some other confinement principles have been investigated.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antimatter_catalyzed_nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Antimatter catalyzed nuclear pulse propulsion">Antimatter-initialized fusion</a> uses small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> to trigger a tiny fusion explosion. This has been studied primarily in the context of making <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Nuclear pulse propulsion">nuclear pulse propulsion</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_fusion_bomb" title="Pure fusion bomb">pure fusion bombs</a> feasible. This is not near becoming a practical power source, due to the cost of manufacturing antimatter alone.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pyroelectric_fusion" title="Pyroelectric fusion">Pyroelectric fusion</a> was reported in April 2005 by a team at <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Los_Angeles" title="University of California, Los Angeles">UCLA</a>. The scientists used a <a href="/wiki/Pyroelectricity" title="Pyroelectricity">pyroelectric</a> crystal heated from &#8722;34 to 7&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;29 to 45&#160;&#176;F), combined with a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> needle to produce an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> of about 25&#160;gigavolts per meter to ionize and accelerate <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> nuclei into an <a href="/wiki/Erbium" title="Erbium">erbium</a> deuteride target.  At the estimated energy levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion_reactions" title="Nuclear fusion reactions">D-D fusion reaction</a> may occur, producing <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> and a 2.45&#160;MeV <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>. Although it makes a useful neutron generator, the apparatus is not intended for power generation since it requires far more energy than it produces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion-fission_hybrid" title="Nuclear fusion-fission hybrid">Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission (hybrid nuclear power)</a> is a proposed means of generating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" title="Electrical power industry">power</a>  by use of a combination of nuclear fusion and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> processes. The concept dates to the 1950s, and was briefly advocated by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to the delays in the realization of pure fusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-24">[24]</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Project_PACER" title="Project PACER">Project PACER</a>, carried out at <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a> (LANL) in the mid-1970s, explored the possibility of a fusion power system that would involve exploding small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H-bomb" title="H-bomb">hydrogen bombs</a> (fusion bombs) inside an underground cavity. As an energy source, the system is the only fusion power system that could be demonstrated to work using existing technology. However it would also require a large, continuous supply of nuclear bombs, making the economics of such a system rather questionable.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_reactions">Important reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Important reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astrophysical_reaction_chains">Astrophysical reaction chains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Astrophysical reaction chains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the temperatures and densities in stellar cores the rates of fusion reactions are notoriously slow. For example, at solar core temperature (<i>T</i> &#8776; 15&#160;MK) and density (160&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>), the energy release rate is only 276&#160;&#956;W/cm<sup>3</sup>&#8212;about a quarter of the volumetric rate at which a resting human body generates heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Thus, reproduction of stellar core conditions in a lab for nuclear fusion power production is completely impractical. Because nuclear reaction rates depend on density as well as temperature and most fusion schemes operate at relatively low densities, those methods are strongly dependent on higher temperatures. The fusion rate as a function of temperature (exp(&#8722;<i>E</i>/<i>kT</i>)), leads to the need to achieve temperatures in terrestrial reactors 10&#8211;100 times higher temperatures than in stellar interiors: <i>T</i> &#8776; 0.1&#8211;1.0&#215;10<sup>9</sup> K.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criteria_and_candidates_for_terrestrial_reactions">Criteria and candidates for terrestrial reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_fusion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Criteria and candidates for terrestrial reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power#Fuels" title="Fusion power">Fusion power &#167;&#160;Fuels</a></div>\n<p>In artificial fusion, the primary fuel is not constrained to be protons and higher temperatures can be used, so reactions with larger cross-sections are chosen. Another concern is the production of neutrons, which activate the reactor structure radiologically, but also have the advantages of allowing volumetric extraction of the fusion energy and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> breeding. Reactions that release no neutrons are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Aneutronic_fusion" title="Aneutronic fusion"><i>aneutronic</i></a>.\n</p><p>To be a useful energy source, a fusion reaction must satisfy several criteria. It must:\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a></dt>\n<dd>This limits the reactants to the low <i>Z</i> (number of protons) side of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy#Nuclear_binding_energy_curve" title="Nuclear binding energy">curve of binding energy</a>. It also makes helium <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> the most common product because of its extraordinarily tight binding, although <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-3" title="Hydrogen-3"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> also show up.</dd>\n<dt>Involve low atomic number (<i>Z</i>) nuclei</dt>\n<dd>This is because the electrostatic repulsion that must be overcome before the nuclei are close enough to fuse is directly related to the number of protons it contains - its atomic number.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This implies a relationship between atomic number (Z) and activation energy (in addition to its relation to binding energy), which isn\'t established/cited here. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></dd>\n<dt>Have two reactants</dt>\n<dd>At anything less than stellar densities, three body collisions are too improbable. In inertial confinement, both stellar densities and temperatures are exceeded to compensate for the shortcomings of the third parameter of the Lawson criterion, ICF\'s very short confinement time.</dd>\n<dt>Have two or more products</dt>\n<dd>This allows simultaneous conservation of energy and momentum without relying on the electromagnetic force.</dd>\n<dt>Conserve both protons and neutrons</dt>\n<dd>The cross sections for the weak interaction are too small.</dd></dl>\n<p>Few reactions meet these criteria. The following are those with the largest cross sections:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><table border="0">\n<tbody><tr style="height:2em;">\n<td>(1)&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>&#160;</td>\n<td>+&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>T<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>&#160;</td>\n<td>&#8594;&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>&#160;</td>\n<td>(&#160;</td>\n<td>3.5&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">MeV</a>&#160;</td>\n<td>)&#160;</td>\n<td>+&#160;</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">n<sup>0</sup></a>&#160;</td>\n<td>(&#160;</td>\n<td>14.1&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">MeV</a>&#160;</td>\n<td>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="height:2em;">\n<td>(2i)&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>&#160;</td>\n<td>+&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>&#160;</td>\n<td>&#8594;&#160;</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>T<span
